Many violations of this rule (as the first three below) arise from faulty arrangement; others (as the last) from the use of unlike constructions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eIt was both a long ceremony and very tedious.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThe ceremony was both long and tedious.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eA time not for words, but action.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eA time not for words, but for action.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eEither you must grant his request or incur his ill will.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eYou must either grant his request or incur his ill will.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eMy objections are, first, the injustice of the measure; second, that it is unconstitutional.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eMy objections are, first, that the measure is unjust; second, that it is unconstitutional.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSee also the third example under Rule 12 and the last under Rule 13.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIt may be asked, what if a writer needs to express a very large number of similar ideas, say twenty? Must he write twenty consecutive sentences of the same pattern? On closer examination he will probably find that the difficulty is imaginary, that his twenty ideas can be classified in groups, and that he need apply the principle only within each group. Otherwise he had best avoid difficulty by putting his statements in the form of a table.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eRule 16. Keep related words together.\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-rule-16-keep-related-words-together\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Rule 16. Keep related words together.\" href=\"#rule-16-keep-related-words-together\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe position of the words in a sentence is the principal means of showing their relationship. The writer must therefore, so far as possible, bring together the words, and groups of words, that are related in thought, and keep apart those which are not so related.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe subject of a sentence and the principal verb should not, as a rule, be separated by a phrase or clause that can be transferred to the beginning.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eWordsworth, in the fifth book of The Excursion, gives a minute description of this church.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eIn the fifth book of The Excursion, Wordsworth gives a minute description of this church.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eCast iron, when treated in a Bessemer converter, is changed into steel.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eBy treatment in a Bessemer converter, cast iron is changed into steel.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe objection is that the interposed phrase or clause needlessly interrupts the natural order of the main clause. Usually, however, this objection does not hold when the order is interrupted only by a relative clause or by an expression in apposition. Nor does it hold in periodic sentences in which the interruption is a deliberately used means of creating suspense (see examples under Rule 18).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe relative pronoun should come, as a rule, immediately after its antecedent.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThere was a look in his eye that boded mischief.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eIn his eye was a look that boded mischief.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe wrote three articles about his adventures in Spain, which were published in Harper's Magazine.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe published in Harper's Magazine three articles about his adventures in Spain.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThis is a portrait of Benjamin Harrison, grandson of William Henry Harrison, who became President in 1889.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThis is a portrait of Benjamin Harrison, grandson of William Henry Harrison. He became President in 1889.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIf the antecedent consists of a group of words, the relative comes at the end of the group, unless this would cause ambiguity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Superintendent of the Chicago Division, who\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eA proposal to amend the Sherman Act, which has been variously judged.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eA proposal, which has been variously judged, to amend the Sherman Act.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003e—\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eA proposal to amend the much-debated Sherman Act.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThe grandson of William Henry Harrison, who\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eWilliam Henry Harrison's grandson, who\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eA noun in apposition may come between antecedent and relative, because in such a combination no real ambiguity can arise.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Duke of York, his brother, who was regarded with hostility by the Whigs\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eModifiers should come, if possible, next to the word they modify. If several expressions modify the same word, they should be so arranged that no wrong relation is suggested.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eAll the members were not present.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eNot all the members were present.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe only found two mistakes.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe found only two mistakes.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eMajor R. E. Joyce will give a lecture on Tuesday evening in Bailey Hall, to which the public is invited, on “My Experiences in Mesopotamia” at eight P. M.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eOn Tuesday evening at eight P. M., Major R. E. Joyce will give in Bailey Hall a lecture on “My Experiences in Mesopotamia.” The public is invited.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eRule 17. In summaries, keep to one tense.\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-rule-17-in-summaries-keep-to-one-tense\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Rule 17. In summaries, keep to one tense.\" href=\"#rule-17-in-summaries-keep-to-one-tense\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn summarizing the action of a drama, the writer should always use the present tense. In summarizing a poem, story, or novel, he should preferably use the present, though he may use the past if he prefers. If the summary is in the present tense, antecedent action should be expressed by the perfect; if in the past, by the past perfect.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAn unforeseen chance prevents Friar John from delivering Friar Lawrence's letter to Romeo. Meanwhile, owing to her father's arbitrary change of the day set for her wedding, Juliet has been compelled to drink the potion on Tuesday night, with the result that Balthasar informs Romeo of her supposed death before Friar Lawrence learns of the non-delivery of the letter.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eBut whichever tense be used in the summary, a past tense in indirect discourse or in indirect question remains unchanged.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Friar confesses that it was he who married them.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eApart from the exceptions noted, whichever tense the writer chooses, he should use throughout. Shifting from one tense to the other gives the appearance of uncertainty and irresolution (compare Rule 15).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn presenting the statements or the thought of some one else, as in summarizing an essay or reporting a speech, the writer should avoid intercalating such expressions as “he said,” “he stated,” “the speaker added,” “the speaker then went on to say,” “the author also thinks,” or the like. He should indicate clearly at the outset, once for all, that what follows is summary, and then waste no words in repeating the notification.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn notebooks, in newspapers, in handbooks of literature, summaries of one kind or another may be indispensable, and for children in primary schools it is a useful exercise to retell a story in their own words. But in the criticism or interpretation of literature the writer should be careful to avoid dropping into summary. He may find it necessary to devote one or two sentences to indicating the subject, or the opening situation, of the work he is discussing; he may cite numerous details to illustrate its qualities. But he should aim to write an orderly discussion supported by evidence, not a summary with occasional comment. Similarly, if the scope of his discussion includes a number of works, he will as a rule do better not to take them up singly in chronological order, but to aim from the beginning at establishing general conclusions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eRule 18. Place the emphatic words of a sentence at the end.\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-rule-18-place-the-emphatic-words-of-a-sentence-at-the-end\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Rule 18. Place the emphatic words of a sentence at the end.\" href=\"#rule-18-place-the-emphatic-words-of-a-sentence-at-the-end\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe proper place in the sentence for the word, or group of words, which the writer desires to make most prominent is usually the end.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHumanity has hardly advanced in fortitude since that time, though it has advanced in many other ways.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHumanity, since that time, has advanced in many other ways, but it has hardly advanced in fortitude.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThis steel is principally used for making razors, because of its hardness.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eBecause of its hardness, this steel is principally used in making razors.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe word or group of words entitled to this position of prominence is usually the logical predicate, that is, the \u003cem\u003enew\u003c/em\u003e element in the sentence, as it is in the second example.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe effectiveness of the periodic sentence arises from the prominence which it gives to the main statement.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eFour centuries ago, Christopher Columbus, one of the Italian mariners whom the decline of their own republics had put at the service of the world and of adventure, seeking for Spain a westward passage to the Indies as a set-off against the achievements of Portuguese discoverers, lighted on America.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWith these hopes and in this belief I would urge you, laying aside all hindrance, thrusting away all private aims, to devote yourself unswervingly and unflinchingly to the vigorous and successful prosecution of this war.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe other prominent position in the sentence is the beginning. Any element in the sentence, other than the subject, may become emphatic when placed first.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eDeceit or treachery he could never forgive.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo vast and rude, fretted by the action of nearly three thousand years, the fragments of this architecture may often seem, at first sight, like works of nature.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eA subject coming first in its sentence may be emphatic, but hardly by its position alone. In the sentence,\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eGreat kings worshipped at his shrine,\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003ethe emphasis upon \u003cem\u003ekings\u003c/em\u003e arises largely from its meaning and from the context. To receive special emphasis, the subject of a sentence must take the position of the predicate.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThrough the middle of the valley flowed a winding stream.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe principle that the proper place for what is to be made most prominent is the end applies equally to the words of a sentence, to the sentences of a paragraph, and to the paragraphs of a composition.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch2 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eV. Words And Expressions Commonly Misused\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-v-words-and-expressions-commonly-misused\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: V. Words And Expressions Commonly Misused\" href=\"#v-words-and-expressions-commonly-misused\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e(Some of the forms here listed, as \u003cem\u003elike I did\u003c/em\u003e, are downright bad English; others, as the split infinitive, have their defenders, but are in such general disfavor that it is at least inadvisable to use them; still others, as \u003cem\u003ecase\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003efactor\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003efeature\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003einteresting\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eone of the most\u003c/em\u003e, are good in their place, but are constantly obtruding themselves into places where they have no right to be. If the writer will make it his purpose from the beginning to express accurately his own individual thought, and will refuse to be satisfied with a ready-made formula that saves him the trouble of doing so, this last set of expressions will cause him little trouble. But if he finds that in a moment of inadvertence he has used one of them, his proper course will probably be not to patch up the sentence by substituting one word or set of words for another, but to recast it completely, as illustrated in a number of examples below and in others under Rules 12 and 13.)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAll right.\u003c/strong\u003e Idiomatic in familiar speech as a detached phrase in the sense, “Agreed,” or “Go ahead.” In other uses better avoided. Always written as two words.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAs good or better than.\u003c/strong\u003e Expressions of this type should be corrected by rearranging the sentence.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eMy opinion is as good or better than his.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eMy opinion is as good as his, or better (if not better).\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAs to whether.\u003c/strong\u003e \u003cem\u003eWhether\u003c/em\u003e is sufficient; see under Rule 13.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBid.\u003c/strong\u003e Takes the infinitive without \u003cem\u003eto\u003c/em\u003e. The past tense in the sense, \u003cem\u003e“ordered,”\u003c/em\u003e is \u003cem\u003ebade\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBut.\u003c/strong\u003e Unnecessary after \u003cem\u003edoubt\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003ehelp\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eI have no doubt but that\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eI have no doubt that\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe could not help see but that\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe could not help seeing that\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe too frequent use of \u003cem\u003ebut\u003c/em\u003e as a conjunction leads to the fault discussed under Rule 14. A loose sentence formed with \u003cem\u003ebut\u003c/em\u003e can always be converted into a periodic sentence formed with \u003cem\u003ealthough\u003c/em\u003e, as illustrated under Rule 4.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eParticularly awkward is the following of one \u003cem\u003ebut\u003c/em\u003e by another, making a contrast to a contrast or a reservation to a reservation. This is easily corrected by re-arrangement.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eAmerica had vast resources, but she seemed almost wholly unprepared for war. But within a year she had created an army of four million men.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eAmerica seemed almost wholly unprepared for war, but she had vast resources. Within a year she had created an army of four million men.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCan.\u003c/strong\u003e Means \u003cem\u003eam (is, are) able\u003c/em\u003e. Not to be used as a substitute for \u003cem\u003emay\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCase.\u003c/strong\u003e The Concise Oxford Dictionary begins its definition of this word: “instance of a thing's occurring; usual state of affairs.” In these two senses, the word is usually unnecessary.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eIn many cases, the rooms were poorly ventilated.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eMany of the rooms were poorly ventilated.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eIt has rarely been the case that any mistake has been made.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eFew mistakes have been made.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSee Wood, Suggestions to Authors, pp. 68–71, and Quiller-Couch, The Art of Writing, pp. 103–106.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCertainly.\u003c/strong\u003e Used indiscriminately by some writers, much as others use \u003cem\u003every\u003c/em\u003e, to intensify any and every statement. A mannerism of this kind, bad in speech, is even worse in writing.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCharacter.\u003c/strong\u003e Often simply redundant, used from a mere habit of wordiness.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eActs of a hostile character\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHostile acts\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eClaim, vb.\u003c/strong\u003e With object-noun, means \u003cem\u003elay claim to\u003c/em\u003e. May be used with a dependent clause if this sense is clearly involved: “He claimed that he was the sole surviving heir.” (But even here, “claimed to be” would be better.) Not to be used as a substitute for \u003cem\u003edeclare\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003emaintain\u003c/em\u003e, or \u003cem\u003echarge\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eClever.\u003c/strong\u003e This word has been greatly overused; it is best restricted to ingenuity displayed in small matters.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCompare.\u003c/strong\u003e To \u003cem\u003ecompare to\u003c/em\u003e is to point out or imply resemblances, between objects regarded as essentially of different order; to \u003cem\u003ecompare with\u003c/em\u003e is mainly to point out differences, between objects regarded as essentially of the same order. Thus life has been compared to a pilgrimage, to a drama, to a battle; Congress may be compared with the British Parliament. Paris has been compared to ancient Athens; it may be compared with modern London.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConsider.\u003c/strong\u003e Not followed by \u003cem\u003eas\u003c/em\u003e when it means “believe to be.” “I consider him thoroughly competent.” Compare, “The lecturer considered Cromwell first as soldier and second as administrator,” where “considered” means “examined” or “discussed.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eData.\u003c/strong\u003e A plural, like \u003cem\u003ephenomena\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003estrata\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThese data were tabulated.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDependable.\u003c/strong\u003e A needless substitute for \u003cem\u003ereliable\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003etrustworthy\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDifferent than.\u003c/strong\u003e Not permissible. Substitute \u003cem\u003edifferent from\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eother than\u003c/em\u003e, or \u003cem\u003eunlike\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDivided into.\u003c/strong\u003e Not to be misused for \u003cem\u003ecomposed of\u003c/em\u003e. The line is sometimes difficult to draw; doubtless plays are divided into acts, but poems are composed of stanzas.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDon't.\u003c/strong\u003e Contraction of \u003cem\u003edo not\u003c/em\u003e. The contraction of \u003cem\u003edoes not\u003c/em\u003e is \u003cem\u003edoesn't\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDue to.\u003c/strong\u003e Incorrectly used for \u003cem\u003ethrough\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003ebecause of\u003c/em\u003e, or \u003cem\u003eowing to\u003c/em\u003e, in adverbial phrases: “He lost the first game, due to carelessness.” In correct use related as predicate or as modifier to a particular noun: “This invention is due to Edison;” “losses due to preventable fires.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFolk.\u003c/strong\u003e A collective noun, equivalent to \u003cem\u003epeople\u003c/em\u003e. Use the singular form only.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eEffect.\u003c/strong\u003e As noun, means \u003cem\u003eresult\u003c/em\u003e; as verb, means \u003cem\u003e\u003cem\u003eto\u003c/em\u003e bring about\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eaccomplish\u003c/em\u003e (not to be confused with \u003cem\u003eaffect\u003c/em\u003e, which means “to influence”).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAs noun, often loosely used in perfunctory writing about fashions, music, painting, and other arts: “an Oriental effect;” “effects in pale green;” “very delicate effects;” “broad effects;” “subtle effects;” “a charming effect was produced by.” The writer who has a definite meaning to express will not take refuge in such vagueness.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eEtc.\u003c/strong\u003e Equivalent to \u003cem\u003eand the rest\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eand so forth\u003c/em\u003e, and hence not to be used if one of these would be insufficient, that is, if the reader would be left in doubt as to any important particulars. Least open to objection when it represents the last terms of a list already given in full, or immaterial words at the end of a quotation.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAt the end of a list introduced by \u003cem\u003esuch as\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003efor example\u003c/em\u003e, or any similar expression, \u003cem\u003eetc.\u003c/em\u003e is incorrect.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFact.\u003c/strong\u003e Use this word only of matters of a kind capable of direct verification, not of matters of judgment. That a particular event happened on a given date, that lead melts at a certain temperature, are facts. But such conclusions as that Napoleon was the greatest of modern generals, or that the climate of California is delightful, however incontestable they \u003cem\u003emay be\u003c/em\u003e, are not properly facts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eOn the formula \u003cem\u003ethe fact that\u003c/em\u003e, see under Rule 13.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFactor.\u003c/strong\u003e A hackneyed word; the expressions of which it forms part can usually be replaced by something more direct and idiomatic.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHis superior training was the great factor in his winning the match.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe won the match by being better trained.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHeavy artillery has become an increasingly important factor in deciding battles.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHeavy artillery has played a constantly larger part in deciding battles.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFeature.\u003c/strong\u003e Another hackneyed word; like \u003cem\u003efactor\u003c/em\u003e it usually adds nothing to the sentence in which it occurs.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eA feature of the entertainment especially worthy of mention was the singing of Miss A.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003e(Better use the same number of words to tell what Miss A. sang, or if the programme has already been given, to tell how she sang.)\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAs a verb, in the advertising sense of \u003cem\u003eoffer as a special attraction\u003c/em\u003e, to be avoided.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFix.\u003c/strong\u003e Colloquial in America for \u003cem\u003earrange\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eprepare\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003emend\u003c/em\u003e. In writing restrict it to its literary senses, \u003cem\u003efasten\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003emake firm or immovable\u003c/em\u003e, etc.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eGet.\u003c/strong\u003e The colloquial \u003cem\u003ehave got\u003c/em\u003e for \u003cem\u003ehave\u003c/em\u003e should not be used in writing. The preferable form of the participle is \u003cem\u003egot\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHe is a man who.\u003c/strong\u003e A common type of redundant expression; see Rule 13.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe is a man who is very ambitious.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eHe is very ambitious.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eSpain is a country which I have always wanted to visit.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eI have always wanted to visit Spain.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHelp.\u003c/strong\u003e See under \u003cstrong\u003eBut\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHowever.\u003c/strong\u003e In the meaning \u003cem\u003enevertheless\u003c/em\u003e, not to come first in its sentence or clause.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThe roads were almost impassable. However, we at last succeeded in reaching camp.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThe roads were almost impassable. At last, however, we succeeded in reaching camp.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhen \u003cem\u003ehowever\u003c/em\u003e comes first, it means \u003cem\u003ein whatever way\u003c/em\u003e or \u003cem\u003eto whatever extent\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eHowever you advise him, he will probably do as he thinks best.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eHowever discouraging the prospect, he never lost heart.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eInteresting.\u003c/strong\u003e Avoid this word as a perfunctory means of introduction. quantity, \u003cem\u003efewer\u003c/em\u003e to number. “His troubles are less than mine” means “His troubles are not so great as mine.” “His troubles are fewer than mine” means “His troubles are not so numerous as mine.” It is, however, correct to say, “The signers of the petition were less than a hundred,” where the round number \u003cem\u003ea hundred\u003c/em\u003e is something like a in the right-hand column, they are verbal nouns (gerunds). The construction shown in the left-hand column is occasionally found, and has its defenders. Yet it is easy to see that the second sentence has to do not with a prospect of the Senate, but with a prospect of accepting. In this example, at least, the construction is plainly illogical.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAs the authors of The King's English point out, there are sentences apparently, but not really, of this type, in which the possessive is not called for.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eI cannot imagine Lincoln refusing his assent to this measure.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn this sentence, what the writer cannot imagine is Lincoln himself, in the act of refusing his assent. Yet the meaning would be virtually the same, except for a slight loss of vividness, if he had written,\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eI cannot imagine Lincoln's refusing his assent to this measure.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eBy using the possessive, the writer will always be on the safe side.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn the examples above, the subject of the action is a single, unmodified term, immediately preceding the verbal noun, and the construction is as good as any that could be used. But in any sentence in which it is a mere clumsy substitute for something simpler, or in which the use of the possessive is awkward or impossible, should of course be recast.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eIn the event of a reconsideration of the whole matter's becoming necessary\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eIf it should become necessary to reconsider the whole matter\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThere was great dissatisfaction with the decision of the arbitrators being favorable to the company.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThere was great dissatisfaction that the arbitrators should have decided in favor of the company.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePeople.\u003c/strong\u003e \u003cem\u003eThe people\u003c/em\u003e is a political term, not to be confused with \u003cem\u003ethe public\u003c/em\u003e. dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eShall, Will.\u003c/strong\u003e The future tense requires \u003cem\u003eshall\u003c/em\u003e for the first person, \u003cem\u003ewill\u003c/em\u003e for the second and third. The formula to express the speaker's belief regarding his future action or state is \u003cem\u003eI shall\u003c/em\u003e; \u003cem\u003eI will\u003c/em\u003e expresses his determination or his consent.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eShould.\u003c/strong\u003e See under \u003cstrong\u003eWould\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSo.\u003c/strong\u003e Avoid, in writing, the use of \u003cem\u003eso\u003c/em\u003e as an intensifier: “so good;” “so warm;” “so delightful.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eOn the use of \u003cem\u003eso\u003c/em\u003e to introduce clauses, see Rule 4.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSort of.\u003c/strong\u003e See under \u003cstrong\u003eKind of\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSplit Infinitive.\u003c/strong\u003e There is precedent from the fourteenth century downward for interposing an adverb between \u003cem\u003eto\u003c/em\u003e and the infinitive which it governs, but the construction is in disfavor and is avoided by nearly all careful writers.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eTo diligently inquire\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eTo inquire diligently\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eState.\u003c/strong\u003e Not to be used as a mere substitute for \u003cem\u003esay\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eremark\u003c/em\u003e. Restrict it to the sense of \u003cem\u003eexpress fully or clearly\u003c/em\u003e, as, “He refused to state his objections.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eStudent Body.\u003c/strong\u003e A needless and awkward expression meaning no more than the simple word \u003cem\u003estudents\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eA resolutions.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSystem.\u003c/strong\u003e Frequently used without need.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eDayton has adopted the commission system of \u003cem\u003egovernment.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eDayton has adopted government by commission.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThe dormitory system\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eDormitories\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThanking You in Advance.\u003c/strong\u003e This sounds as if the writer meant, “It will not be worth my while to write to you again.” In making your request, write, “Will you please,” or “I shall be obliged,” and if anything further seems necessary write a letter of acknowledgment later.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThey.\u003c/strong\u003e A common inaccuracy is the use of the plural pronoun when the antecedent is a distributive expression such as \u003cem\u003eeach\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eeach one\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eeverybody\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eevery one\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003emany a man\u003c/em\u003e, which, though implying more than one person, requires the pronoun to be in the singular. Similar to this, but with even less justification, is the use of the plural pronoun with the antecedent \u003cem\u003eanybody\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eany one\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003esomebody\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003esome one\u003c/em\u003e, the intention being either to avoid the awkward “he or she,” or to avoid committing oneself to either. Some bashful speakers even say, “A friend of mine told me that they, etc.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eUse \u003cem\u003ehe\u003c/em\u003e with all the above words, unless the antecedent is or must be feminine.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eVery.\u003c/strong\u003e Use this word sparingly. Where emphasis is necessary, use words strong in themselves.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eViewpoint.\u003c/strong\u003e Write \u003cem\u003epoint of view\u003c/em\u003e, but do not misuse this, as many do, for \u003cem\u003eview\u003c/em\u003e or \u003cem\u003eopinion\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhile.\u003c/strong\u003e Avoid the indiscriminate use of this word for \u003cem\u003eand\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003ebut\u003c/em\u003e, and \u003cem\u003ealthough\u003c/em\u003e. Many writers use it frequently as a substitute for \u003cem\u003eand\u003c/em\u003e or \u003cem\u003ebut\u003c/em\u003e, either from a mere desire to vary the connective, or from uncertainty which of the two connectives is the more appropriate. In this use it is best replaced by a semicolon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eOriginal\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003cth\u003eRevision\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThe office and salesrooms are on the ground floor, while the rest of the building is devoted to manufacturing.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd\u003eThe office and salesrooms are on the ground floor; the rest of the building is devoted to manufacturing.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIts use as a virtual equivalent of \u003cem\u003ealthough\u003c/em\u003e is allowable in sentences where this leads to no ambiguity or absurdity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhile I admire his energy, I wish it were employed in a better cause.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThis is entirely correct, as shown by the paraphrase,\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eI admire his energy; actions</span></div></div></div></div><div></div></div><div class="Box-sc-62in7e-0 iyIiMl"><section aria-labelledby="file-name-id-wide file-name-id-mobile" class="Box-sc-62in7e-0 iZiBDT"><div class="Box-sc-62in7e-0 lhfNqO js-snippet-clipboard-copy-unpositioned undefined" data-hpc="true"><article class="markdown-body entry-content container-lg" itemprop="text"><div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h1 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">The Elements of Style (1918)</h1><a id="user-content-the-elements-of-style-1918" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: The Elements of Style (1918)" href="#the-elements-of-style-1918"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto"><em>Public domain text by William Strunk Jr.</em></p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Contents</h2><a id="user-content-contents" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Contents" href="#contents"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><a href="#i-introductory">I. Introductory</a></li>
<li><a href="#ii-elementary-rules-of-usage">II. Elementary Rules Of Usage</a>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><a href="#rule-1-form-the-possessive-singular-of-nouns-by-adding-s">Rule 1. Form the possessive singular of nouns by adding 's.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-2-in-a-series-of-three-or-more-terms-with-a-single-conjunction-use-a-comma-after-each-term-except-the-last">Rule 2. In a series of three or more terms with a single conjunction, use a comma after each term except the last.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-3-enclose-parenthetic-expressions-between-commas">Rule 3. Enclose parenthetic expressions between commas.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-4-place-a-comma-before-a-conjunction-introducing-a-co-ordinate-clause">Rule 4. Place a comma before a conjunction introducing a co-ordinate clause.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-5-do-not-join-independent-clauses-by-a-comma">Rule 5. Do not join independent clauses by a comma.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-6-do-not-break-sentences-in-two">Rule 6. Do not break sentences in two.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-7-a-participial-phrase-at-the-beginning-of-a-sentence-must-refer-to-the-grammatical-subject">Rule 7. A participial phrase at the beginning of a sentence must refer to the grammatical subject.</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="#iii-elementary-principles-of-composition">III. Elementary Principles Of Composition</a>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><a href="#rule-8-make-the-paragraph-the-unit-of-composition-one-paragraph-to-each-topic">Rule 8. Make the paragraph the unit of composition: one paragraph to each topic.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-9-as-a-rule-begin-each-paragraph-with-a-topic-sentence-end-it-in-conformity-with-the-beginning">Rule 9. As a rule, begin each paragraph with a topic sentence, end it in conformity with the beginning.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-10-use-the-active-voice">Rule 10. Use the active voice.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-11-put-statements-in-positive-form">Rule 11. Put statements in positive form.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-12-use-definite-specific-concrete-language">Rule 12. Use definite, specific, concrete language.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-13-omit-needless-words">Rule 13. Omit needless words.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-14-avoid-a-succession-of-loose-sentences">Rule 14. Avoid a succession of loose sentences</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-15-express-co-ordinate-ideas-in-similar-form">Rule 15. Express co-ordinate ideas in similar form.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-16-keep-related-words-together">Rule 16. Keep related words together.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-17-in-summaries-keep-to-one-tense">Rule 17. In summaries, keep to one tense.</a></li>
<li><a href="#rule-18-place-the-emphatic-words-of-a-sentence-at-the-end">Rule 18. Place the emphatic words of a sentence at the end.</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="#v-words-and-expressions-commonly-misused">V. Words And Expressions Commonly Misused</a></li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">I. Introductory</h2><a id="user-content-i-introductory" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: I. Introductory" href="#i-introductory"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">This handbook summarizes the essentials of plain English style. It focuses on the rules of usage and principles of composition most often broken, offering a compact alternative to exhaustive manuals. Master the guidance here, then look to the best authors for finer points of style.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">II. Elementary Rules Of Usage</h2><a id="user-content-ii-elementary-rules-of-usage" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: II. Elementary Rules Of Usage" href="#ii-elementary-rules-of-usage"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 1. Form the possessive singular of nouns by adding 's.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-1-form-the-possessive-singular-of-nouns-by-adding-s" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 1. Form the possessive singular of nouns by adding 's." href="#rule-1-form-the-possessive-singular-of-nouns-by-adding-s"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Follow this rule whatever the final consonant. Thus write,</p>
<p dir="auto">Charles's friend</p>
<p dir="auto">Burns's poems</p>
<p dir="auto">the witch's malice</p>
<p dir="auto">This is the usage of the United States Government Printing Office and of the Oxford University Press.</p>
<p dir="auto">Exceptions are the possessive of ancient proper names in <em>-es</em> and <em>-is</em>, the possessive <em>Jesus'</em>, and such forms as <em>for conscience' sake</em>, <em>for righteousness' sake</em>. But such forms as <em>Achilles' heel</em>, <em>Moses' laws</em>, <em>Isis' temple</em> are commonly replaced by</p>
<p dir="auto">the heel of Achilles</p>
<p dir="auto">the laws of Moses</p>
<p dir="auto">the temple of Isis</p>
<p dir="auto">The pronominal possessives <em>hers</em>, <em>its</em>, <em>theirs</em>, <em>yours</em>, and <em>oneself</em> have no apostrophe.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 2. In a series of three or more terms with a single conjunction, use a comma after each term except the last.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-2-in-a-series-of-three-or-more-terms-with-a-single-conjunction-use-a-comma-after-each-term-except-the-last" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 2. In a series of three or more terms with a single conjunction, use a comma after each term except the last." href="#rule-2-in-a-series-of-three-or-more-terms-with-a-single-conjunction-use-a-comma-after-each-term-except-the-last"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Thus write,</p>
<p dir="auto">red, white, and blue</p>
<p dir="auto">gold, silver, or copper</p>
<p dir="auto">He opened the letter, read it, and made a note of its contents.</p>
<p dir="auto">This is also the usage of the Government Printing Office and of the Oxford University Press.</p>
<p dir="auto">In the names of business firms the last comma is omitted, as,</p>
<p dir="auto">Brown, Shipley &amp; Co.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 3. Enclose parenthetic expressions between commas.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-3-enclose-parenthetic-expressions-between-commas" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 3. Enclose parenthetic expressions between commas." href="#rule-3-enclose-parenthetic-expressions-between-commas"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">The best way to see a country, unless you are pressed for time, is to travel on foot.</p>
<p dir="auto">This rule is difficult to apply; it is frequently hard to decide whether a single word, such as <em>however</em>, or a brief phrase, is or is not parenthetic. If the interruption to the flow of the sentence is but slight, the writer may safely omit the commas. But whether the interruption be slight or considerable, he must never insert one comma and omit the other. Such punctuation as</p>
<p dir="auto">Marjorie's husband, Colonel Nelson paid us a visit yesterday,</p>
<p dir="auto">or</p>
<p dir="auto">My brother you will be pleased to hear, is now in perfect health,</p>
<p dir="auto">is indefensible.</p>
<p dir="auto">If a parenthetic expression is preceded by a conjunction, place the first comma before the conjunction, not after it.</p>
<p dir="auto">He saw us coming, and unaware that we had learned of his treachery, greeted us with a smile.</p>
<p dir="auto">Always to be regarded as parenthetic and to be enclosed between commas (or, at the end of the sentence, between comma and period) are the following:</p>
<p dir="auto">(1) the year, when forming part of a date, and the day of the month, when following the day of the week:</p>
<p dir="auto">February to July, 1916.</p>
<p dir="auto">April 6, 1917.</p>
<p dir="auto">Monday, November 11, 1918.</p>
<p dir="auto">(2) the abbreviations <em>etc.</em> and <em>jr.</em></p>
<p dir="auto">(3) non-restrictive relative clauses, that is, those which do not serve to identify or define the antecedent noun, and similar clauses introduced by conjunctions indicating time or place.</p>
<p dir="auto">The audience, which had at first been indifferent, became more and more interested.</p>
<p dir="auto">In this sentence the clause introduced by <em>which</em> does not serve to tell which of several possible audiences is meant; what audience is in question is supposed to be already known. The clause adds, parenthetically, a statement supplementing that in the main clause. The sentence is virtually a combination of two statements which might have been made independently:</p>
<p dir="auto">The audience had at first been indifferent. It became more and more interested.</p>
<p dir="auto">Compare the restrictive relative clause, not set off by commas, in the sentence,</p>
<p dir="auto">The candidate who best meets these requirements will obtain the place.</p>
<p dir="auto">Here the clause introduced by <em>who</em> does serve to tell which of several possible candidates is meant; the sentence cannot be split up into two independent statements.</p>
<p dir="auto">The difference in punctuation in the two sentences following is based on the same principle:</p>
<p dir="auto">Nether Stowey, where Coleridge wrote The Rime of the Ancient Mariner, is a few miles from Bridgewater.</p>
<p dir="auto">The day will come when you will admit your mistake.</p>
<p dir="auto">Nether Stowey is completely identified by its name; the statement about Coleridge is therefore supplementary and parenthetic. The <em>day</em> spoken of is identified only by the dependent clause, which is therefore restrictive.</p>
<p dir="auto">Similar in principle to the enclosing of parenthetic expressions between commas is the setting off by commas of phrases or dependent clauses preceding or following the main clause of a sentence.</p>
<p dir="auto">Partly by hard fighting, partly by diplomatic skill, they enlarged their dominions to the east, and rose to royal rank with the possession of Sicily, exchanged afterwards for Sardinia.</p>
<p dir="auto">Other illustrations may be found in sentences quoted under Rules 4, 5, 6, 7, 16, and 18.</p>
<p dir="auto">The writer should be careful not to set off independent clauses by commas: see under Rule 5.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 4. Place a comma before a conjunction introducing a co-ordinate clause.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-4-place-a-comma-before-a-conjunction-introducing-a-co-ordinate-clause" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 4. Place a comma before a conjunction introducing a co-ordinate clause." href="#rule-4-place-a-comma-before-a-conjunction-introducing-a-co-ordinate-clause"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">The early records of the city have disappeared, and the story of its first years can no longer be reconstructed.</p>
<p dir="auto">The situation is perilous, but there is still one chance of escape.</p>
<p dir="auto">Sentences of this type, isolated from their context, may seem to be in need of rewriting. As they make complete sense when the comma is reached, the second clause has the appearance of an afterthought. Further, <em>and</em> is the least specific of connectives. Used between independent clauses, it indicates only that a relation exists between them without defining that relation. In the example above, the relation is that of cause and result. The two sentences might be rewritten:</p>
<p dir="auto">As the early records of the city have disappeared, the story of its first years can no longer be reconstructed.</p>
<p dir="auto">Although the situation is perilous, there is still one chance of escape.</p>
<p dir="auto">Or the subordinate clauses might be replaced by phrases:</p>
<p dir="auto">Owing to the disappearance of the early records of the city, the story of its first years can no longer be reconstructed.</p>
<p dir="auto">In this perilous situation, there is still one chance of escape.</p>
<p dir="auto">But a writer may err by making his sentences too uniformly compact and periodic, and an occasional loose sentence prevents the style from becoming too formal and gives the reader a certain relief. Consequently, loose sentences of the type first quoted are common in easy, unstudied writing. But a writer should be careful not to construct too many of his sentences after this pattern (see Rule 14).</p>
<p dir="auto">Two-part sentences of which the second member is introduced by <em>as</em> (in the sense of <em>because</em>), <em>for</em>, <em>or</em>, <em>nor</em>, and <em>while</em> (in the sense of <em>and at the same time</em>) likewise require a comma before the conjunction.</p>
<p dir="auto">If the second member is introduced by an adverb, a semicolon, not a comma, is required (see Rule 5). The connectives <em>so</em> and <em>yet</em> may be used either as adverbs or as conjunctions, accordingly as the second clause is felt to be co-ordinate or subordinate; consequently either mark of punctuation may be justified. But these uses of <em>so</em> (equivalent to <em>accordingly</em> or to <em>so that</em>) are somewhat colloquial and should, as a rule, be avoided in writing. A simple correction, usually serviceable, is to omit the word <em>so</em> and begin the first clause with <em>as</em> or <em>since</em>:</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>I had never been in the place before; so I had difficulty in finding my way about.</td>
<td>As I had never been in the place before, I had difficulty in finding my way about.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">If a dependent clause, or an introductory phrase requiring to be set off by a comma, precedes the second independent clause, no comma is needed after the conjunction.</p>
<p dir="auto">The situation is perilous, but if we are prepared to act promptly, there is still one chance of escape.</p>
<p dir="auto">When the subject is the same for both clauses and is expressed only once, a comma is required if the connective is <em>but</em>. If the connective is <em>and</em>, the comma should be omitted if the relation between the two statements is close or immediate.</p>
<p dir="auto">I have heard his arguments, but am still unconvinced.</p>
<p dir="auto">He has had several years' experience and is thoroughly competent.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 5. Do not join independent clauses by a comma.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-5-do-not-join-independent-clauses-by-a-comma" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 5. Do not join independent clauses by a comma." href="#rule-5-do-not-join-independent-clauses-by-a-comma"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">If two or more clauses, grammatically complete and not joined by a conjunction, are to form a single compound sentence, the proper mark of punctuation is a semicolon.</p>
<p dir="auto">Stevenson's romances are entertaining; they are full of exciting adventures.</p>
<p dir="auto">It is nearly half past five; we cannot reach town before dark.</p>
<p dir="auto">It is of course equally correct to write the above as two sentences each, replacing the semicolons by periods.</p>
<p dir="auto">Stevenson's romances are entertaining. They are full of exciting adventures.</p>
<p dir="auto">It is nearly half past five. We cannot reach town before dark.</p>
<p dir="auto">If a conjunction is inserted the proper mark is a comma (Rule 4).</p>
<p dir="auto">Stevenson's romances are entertaining, for they are full of exciting adventures.</p>
<p dir="auto">It is nearly half past five, and we cannot reach town before dark.</p>
<p dir="auto">A comparison of the three forms given above will show clearly the advantage of the first. It is, at least in the examples given, better than the second form, because it suggests the close relationship between the two statements in a way that the second does not attempt, and better than the third, because briefer and therefore more forcible. Indeed it may be said that this simple method of indicating relationship between statements is one of the most useful devices of composition. The relationship, as above, is commonly one of cause or of consequence.</p>
<p dir="auto">Note that if the second clause is preceded by an adverb, such as <em>accordingly</em>, <em>besides</em>, <em>then</em>, <em>therefore</em>, or <em>thus</em>, and not by a conjunction, the semicolon is still required.</p>
<p dir="auto">Two exceptions to the rule may be admitted. If the clauses are very short, and are alike in form, a comma is usually permissible:</p>
<p dir="auto">Man proposes, God disposes.</p>
<p dir="auto">The gate swung apart, the bridge fell, the portcullis was drawn up.</p>
<p dir="auto">Note that in these examples the relation is not one of cause or consequence. Also in the colloquial form of expression,</p>
<p dir="auto">I hardly knew him, he was so changed,</p>
<p dir="auto">a comma, not a semicolon, is required. But this form of expression is inappropriate in writing, except in the dialogue of a story or play, or perhaps in a familiar letter.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 6. Do not break sentences in two.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-6-do-not-break-sentences-in-two" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 6. Do not break sentences in two." href="#rule-6-do-not-break-sentences-in-two"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">In other words, do not use periods for commas.</p>
<p dir="auto">I met them on a Cunard liner several years ago. Coming home from Liverpool to New York.</p>
<p dir="auto">He was an interesting talker. A man who had traveled all over the world and lived in half a dozen countries.</p>
<p dir="auto">In both these examples, the first period should be replaced by a comma, and the following word begun with a small letter.</p>
<p dir="auto">It is permissible to make an emphatic word or expression serve the purpose of a sentence and to punctuate it accordingly:</p>
<p dir="auto">Again and again he called out. No reply.</p>
<p dir="auto">The writer must, however, be certain that the emphasis is warranted, and that he will not be suspected of a mere blunder in syntax or in punctuation.</p>
<p dir="auto">Rules 3, 4, 5, and 6 cover the most important principles in the punctuation of ordinary sentences; they should be so thoroughly mastered that their application becomes second nature.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 7. A participial phrase at the beginning of a sentence must refer to the grammatical subject.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-7-a-participial-phrase-at-the-beginning-of-a-sentence-must-refer-to-the-grammatical-subject" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 7. A participial phrase at the beginning of a sentence must refer to the grammatical subject." href="#rule-7-a-participial-phrase-at-the-beginning-of-a-sentence-must-refer-to-the-grammatical-subject"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Walking slowly down the road, he saw a woman accompanied by two children.</p>
<p dir="auto">The word <em>walking</em> refers to the subject of the sentence, not to the woman. If the writer wishes to make it refer to the woman, he must recast the sentence:</p>
<p dir="auto">He saw a woman accompanied by two children, walking slowly down the road.</p>
<p dir="auto">Participial phrases preceded by a conjunction or by a preposition, nouns in apposition, adjectives, and adjective phrases come under the same rule if they begin the sentence.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>On arriving in Chicago, his friends met him at the station.</td>
<td>When he arrived (or, On his arrival) in Chicago, his friends met him at the station.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>A soldier of proved valor, they entrusted him with the defence of the city.</td>
<td>A soldier of proved valor, he was entrusted with the defence of the city.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Young and inexperienced, the task seemed easy to me.</td>
<td>Young and inexperienced, I thought the task easy.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Without a friend to counsel him, the temptation proved irresistible.</td>
<td>Without a friend to counsel him, he found the temptation irresistible.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">Sentences violating this rule are often ludicrous.</p>
<p dir="auto">Being in a dilapidated condition, I was able to buy the house very cheap.</p>
<p dir="auto">Wondering irresolutely what to do next, the clock struck twelve.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">III. Elementary Principles Of Composition</h2><a id="user-content-iii-elementary-principles-of-composition" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: III. Elementary Principles Of Composition" href="#iii-elementary-principles-of-composition"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 8. Make the paragraph the unit of composition: one paragraph to each topic.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-8-make-the-paragraph-the-unit-of-composition-one-paragraph-to-each-topic" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 8. Make the paragraph the unit of composition: one paragraph to each topic." href="#rule-8-make-the-paragraph-the-unit-of-composition-one-paragraph-to-each-topic"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">If the subject on which you are writing is of slight extent, or if you intend to treat it very briefly, there may be no need of subdividing it into topics. Thus a brief description, a brief summary of a literary work, a brief account of a single incident, a narrative merely outlining an action, the setting forth of a single idea, any one of these is best written in a single paragraph. After the paragraph has been written, examine it to see whether subdivision will not improve it.</p>
<p dir="auto">Ordinarily, however, a subject requires subdivision into topics, each of which should be made the subject of a paragraph. The object of treating each topic in a paragraph by itself is, of course, to aid the reader. The beginning of each paragraph is a signal to him that a new step in the development of the subject has been reached.</p>
<p dir="auto">The extent of subdivision will vary with the length of the composition. For example, a short notice of a book or poem might consist of a single paragraph. One slightly longer might consist of two paragraphs:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>A. Account of the work.</li>
<li>B. Critical discussion.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">A report on a poem, written for a class in literature, might consist of seven paragraphs:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>A. Facts of composition and publication.</li>
<li>B. Kind of poem; metrical form.</li>
<li>C. Subject.</li>
<li>D. Treatment of subject.</li>
<li>E. For what chiefly remarkable.</li>
<li>F. Wherein characteristic of the writer.</li>
<li>G. Relationship to other works.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">The contents of paragraphs C and D would vary with the poem. Usually, paragraph C would indicate the actual or imagined circumstances of the poem (the situation), if these call for explanation, and would then state the subject and outline its development. If the poem is a narrative in the third person throughout, paragraph C need contain no more than a concise summary of the action. Paragraph D would indicate the leading ideas and show how they are made prominent, or would indicate what points in the narrative are chiefly emphasized.</p>
<p dir="auto">A novel might be discussed under the heads:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>A. Setting.</li>
<li>B. Plot.</li>
<li>C. Characters.</li>
<li>D. Purpose.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">An historical event might be discussed under the heads:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>A. What led up to the event.</li>
<li>B. Account of the event.</li>
<li>C. What the event led up to.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">In treating either of these last two subjects, the writer would probably find it necessary to subdivide one or more of the topics here given.</p>
<p dir="auto">As a rule, single sentences should not be written or printed as paragraphs. An exception may be made of sentences of transition, indicating the relation between the parts of an exposition or argument. Frequent exceptions are also necessary in textbooks, guidebooks, and other works in which many topics are treated briefly.</p>
<p dir="auto">In dialogue, each speech, even if only a single word, is a paragraph by itself; that is, a new paragraph begins with each change of speaker. The application of this rule, when dialogue and narrative are combined, is best learned from examples in well-printed works of fiction.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 9. As a rule, begin each paragraph with a topic sentence, end it in conformity with the beginning.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-9-as-a-rule-begin-each-paragraph-with-a-topic-sentence-end-it-in-conformity-with-the-beginning" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 9.
