As a rule, begin each paragraph with a topic sentence, end it in conformity with the beginning." href="#rule-9-as-a-rule-begin-each-paragraph-with-a-topic-sentence-end-it-in-conformity-with-the-beginning"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Again, the object is to aid the reader. The practice here recommended enables him to discover the purpose of each paragraph as he begins to read it, and to retain this purpose in mind as he ends it. For this reason, the most generally useful kind of paragraph, particularly in exposition and argument, is that in which</p>
<p dir="auto">(a) the topic sentence comes at or near the beginning;</p>
<p dir="auto">(b) the succeeding sentences explain or establish or develop the statement made in the topic sentence; and</p>
<p dir="auto">(c) the final sentence either emphasizes the thought of the topic sentence or states some important consequence.</p>
<p dir="auto">Ending with a digression, or with an unimportant detail, is particularly to be avoided.</p>
<p dir="auto">If the paragraph forms part of a larger composition, its relation to what precedes, or its function as a part of the whole, may need to be expressed. This can sometimes be done by a mere word or phrase (<em>again</em>; <em>therefore</em>; <em>for the same reason</em>) in the topic sentence. Sometimes, however, it is expedient to precede the topic sentence by one or more sentences of introduction or transition. If more than one such sentence is required, it is generally better to set apart the transitional sentences as a separate paragraph.</p>
<p dir="auto">According to the writer's purpose, he may, as indicated above, relate the body of the paragraph to the topic sentence in one or more of several different ways. He may make the meaning of the topic sentence clearer by restating it in other forms, by defining its terms, by denying the contrary, by giving illustrations or specific instances; he may establish it by proofs; or he may develop it by showing its implications and consequences. In a long paragraph, he may carry out several of these processes.</p>
<p dir="auto">1 Now, to be properly enjoyed, a walking tour should be gone upon alone. 2 If you go in a company, or even in pairs, it is no longer a walking tour in anything but name; it is something else and more in the nature of a picnic. 3 A walking tour should be gone upon alone, because freedom is of the essence; because you should be able to stop and go on, and follow this way or that, as the freak takes you; and because you must have your own pace, and neither trot alongside a champion walker, nor mince in time with a girl. 4 And you must be open to all impressions and let your thoughts take colour from what you see. 5 You should be as a pipe for any wind to play upon. 6 “I cannot see the wit,” says Hazlitt, “of walking and talking at the same time. 7 When I am in the country, I wish to vegetate like the country,” which is the gist of all that can be said upon the matter. 8 There should be no cackle of voices at your elbow, to jar on the meditative silence of the morning. 9 And so long as a man is reasoning he cannot surrender himself to that fine intoxication that comes of much motion in the open air, that begins in a sort of dazzle and sluggishness of the brain, and ends in a peace that passes comprehension.—Stevenson, Walking Tours.</p>
<p dir="auto">1 Topic sentence. 2 The meaning made clearer by denial of the contrary. 3 The topic sentence repeated, in abridged form, and supported by three reasons; the meaning of the third (“you must have your own pace”) made clearer by denying the contrary. 4 A fourth reason, stated in two forms. 5 The same reason, stated in still another form. 6–7 The same reason as stated by Hazlitt. 8 Repetition, in paraphrase, of the quotation from Hazlitt. 9 Final statement of the fourth reason, in language amplified and heightened to form a strong conclusion.</p>
<p dir="auto">1 It was chiefly in the eighteenth century that a very different conception of history grew up. 2 Historians then came to believe that their task was not so much to paint a picture as to solve a problem; to explain or illustrate the successive phases of national growth, prosperity, and adversity. 3 The history of morals, of industry, of intellect, and of art; the changes that take place in manners or beliefs; the dominant ideas that prevailed in successive periods; the rise, fall, and modification of political constitutions; in a word, all the conditions of national well-being became the subject of their works. 4 They sought rather to write a history of peoples than a history of kings. 5 They looked especially in history for the chain of causes and effects. 6 They undertook to study in the past the physiology of nations, and hoped by applying the experimental method on a large scale to deduce some lessons of real value about the conditions on which the welfare of society mainly depend.—Lecky, The Political Value of History.</p>
<p dir="auto">1 Topic sentence. 2 The meaning of the topic sentence made clearer; the new conception of history defined. 3 The definition expanded. 4 The definition explained by contrast. 5 The definition supplemented: another element in the new conception of history. 6 Conclusion: an important consequence of the new conception of history.</p>
<p dir="auto">In narration and description the paragraph sometimes begins with a concise, comprehensive statement serving to hold together the details that follow.</p>
<p dir="auto">The breeze served us admirably.</p>
<p dir="auto">The campaign opened with a series of reverses.</p>
<p dir="auto">The next ten or twelve pages were filled with a curious set of entries.</p>
<p dir="auto">But this device, if too often used, would become a mannerism. More commonly the opening sentence simply indicates by its subject with what the paragraph is to be principally concerned.</p>
<p dir="auto">At length I thought I might return towards the stockade.</p>
<p dir="auto">He picked up the heavy lamp from the table and began to explore.</p>
<p dir="auto">Another flight of steps, and they emerged on the roof.</p>
<p dir="auto">The brief paragraphs of animated narrative, however, are often without even this semblance of a topic sentence. The break between them serves the purpose of a rhetorical pause, throwing into prominence some detail of the action.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 10. Use the active voice.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-10-use-the-active-voice" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 10. Use the active voice." href="#rule-10-use-the-active-voice"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">The active voice is usually more direct and vigorous than the passive:</p>
<p dir="auto">I shall always remember my first visit to Boston.</p>
<p dir="auto">This is much better than</p>
<p dir="auto">My first visit to Boston will always be remembered by me.</p>
<p dir="auto">The latter sentence is less direct, less bold, and less concise. If the writer tries to make it more concise by omitting “by me,”</p>
<p dir="auto">My first visit to Boston will always be remembered,</p>
<p dir="auto">it becomes indefinite: is it the writer, or some person undisclosed, or the world at large, that will always remember this visit?</p>
<p dir="auto">This rule does not, of course, mean that the writer should entirely discard the passive voice, which is frequently convenient and sometimes necessary.</p>
<p dir="auto">The dramatists of the Restoration are little esteemed to-day.</p>
<p dir="auto">Modern readers have little esteem for the dramatists of the Restoration.</p>
<p dir="auto">The first would be the right form in a paragraph on the dramatists of the Restoration; the second, in a paragraph on the tastes of modern readers. The need of making a particular word the subject of the sentence will often, as in these examples, determine which voice is to be used.</p>
<p dir="auto">As a rule, avoid making one passive depend directly upon another.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Gold was not allowed to be exported.</td>
<td>It was forbidden to export gold (The export of gold was prohibited).</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He has been proved to have been seen entering the building.</td>
<td>It has been proved that he was seen to enter the building.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">In both the examples above, before correction, the word properly related to the second passive is made the subject of the first.</p>
<p dir="auto">A common fault is to use as the subject of a passive construction a noun which expresses the entire action, leaving to the verb no function beyond that of completing the sentence.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>A survey of this region was made in 1900.</td>
<td>This region was surveyed in 1900.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Mobilization of the army was rapidly effected.</td>
<td>The army was rapidly mobilized.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Confirmation of these reports cannot be obtained.</td>
<td>These reports cannot be confirmed.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">Compare the <em>sentence,</em> “The export of gold was prohibited,” in which the predicate “was prohibited” expresses something not implied in “export.”</p>
<p dir="auto">The habitual use of the active voice makes for forcible writing. This is true not only in narrative principally concerned with action, but in writing of any kind. Many a tame sentence of description or exposition can be made lively and emphatic by substituting a verb in the active voice for some such perfunctory expression as <em>there is</em>, or <em>could be heard</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>There were a great number of dead leaves lying on the ground.</td>
<td>Dead leaves covered the ground.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The sound of a guitar somewhere in the house could be heard.</td>
<td>Somewhere in the house a guitar hummed sleepily.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The reason that he left college was that his health became impaired.</td>
<td>Failing health compelled him to leave college.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>It was not long before he was very sorry that he had said what he had.</td>
<td>He soon repented his words.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 11. Put statements in positive form.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-11-put-statements-in-positive-form" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 11. Put statements in positive form." href="#rule-11-put-statements-in-positive-form"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Make definite assertions. Avoid tame, colorless, hesitating, non-committal language. Use the word <em>not</em> as a means of denial or in antithesis, never as a means of evasion.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>He was not very often on time.</td>
<td>He usually came late.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He did not think that studying Latin was much use.</td>
<td>He thought the study of Latin useless.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The Taming of the Shrew is rather weak in spots. Shakespeare does not portray Katharine as a very admirable character, nor does Bianca remain long in memory as an important character in Shakespeare's works.</td>
<td>The women in The Taming of the Shrew are unattractive. Katharine is disagreeable, Bianca insignificant.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">The last example, before correction, is indefinite as well as negative. The corrected version, consequently, is simply a guess at the writer's intention.</p>
<p dir="auto">All three examples show the weakness inherent in the word <em>not</em>. Consciously or unconsciously, the reader is dissatisfied with being told only what is not; he wishes to be told what is. Hence, as a rule, it is better to express even a negative in positive form.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>not honest</td>
<td>dishonest</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>not important</td>
<td>trifling</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>did not remember</td>
<td>forgot</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>did not pay any attention to</td>
<td>ignored</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>did not have much confidence in</td>
<td>distrusted</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">The antithesis of negative and positive is strong:</p>
<p dir="auto">Not charity, but simple justice.</p>
<p dir="auto">Not that I loved Caesar less, but Rome the more.</p>
<p dir="auto">Negative words other than <em>not</em> are usually strong:</p>
<p dir="auto">The sun never sets upon the British flag.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 12. Use definite, specific, concrete language.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-12-use-definite-specific-concrete-language" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 12. Use definite, specific, concrete language." href="#rule-12-use-definite-specific-concrete-language"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Prefer the specific to the general, the definite to the vague, the concrete to the abstract.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>A period of unfavorable weather set in.</td>
<td>It rained every day for a week.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He showed satisfaction as he took possession of his well-earned reward.</td>
<td>He grinned as he pocketed the coin.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>There is a general agreement among those who have enjoyed the experience that surf-riding is productive of great exhilaration.</td>
<td>All who have tried surf-riding agree that it is most exhilarating.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">If those who have studied the art of writing are in accord on any one point, it is on this, that the surest method of arousing and holding the attention of the reader is by being specific, definite, and concrete. Critics have pointed out how much of the effectiveness of the greatest writers, Homer, Dante, Shakespeare, results from their constant definiteness and concreteness. Browning, to cite a more modern author, affords many striking examples. Take, for instance, the lines from My Last Duchess,</p>
<p dir="auto">Sir, 'twas all one! My favour at her breast,</p>
<p dir="auto">The dropping of the daylight in the west,</p>
<p dir="auto">The bough of cherries some officious fool</p>
<p dir="auto">Broke in the orchard for her, the white mule</p>
<p dir="auto">She rode with round the terrace—all and each</p>
<p dir="auto">Would draw from her alike the approving speech,</p>
<p dir="auto">Or blush, at least,</p>
<p dir="auto">and those which end the poem,</p>
<p dir="auto">Notice Neptune, though,</p>
<p dir="auto">Taming a sea-horse, thought a rarity,</p>
<p dir="auto">Which Claus of Innsbruck cast in bronze for me.</p>
<p dir="auto">These words call up pictures. Recall how in The Bishop Orders his Tomb in St. Praxed's Church “the Renaissance spirit—its worldliness, inconsistency, pride, hypocrisy, ignorance of itself, love of art, of luxury, of good Latin,” to quote Ruskin's comment on the poem, is made manifest in specific details and in concrete terms.</p>
<p dir="auto">Prose, in particular narrative and descriptive prose, is made vivid by the same means. If the experiences of Jim Hawkins and of David Balfour, of Kim, of Nostromo, have seemed for the moment real to countless readers, if in reading Carlyle we have almost the sense of being physically present at the taking of the Bastille, it is because of the definiteness of the details and the concreteness of the terms used. It is not that every detail is given; that would be impossible, as well as to no purpose; but that all the significant details are given, and not vaguely, but with such definiteness that the reader, in imagination, can project himself into the scene.</p>
<p dir="auto">In exposition and in argument, the writer must likewise never lose his hold upon the concrete, and even when he is dealing with general principles, he must give particular instances of their application.</p>
<p dir="auto">“This superiority of specific expressions is clearly due to the effort required to translate words into thoughts. As we do not think in generals, but in particulars—as whenever any class of things is referred to, we represent it to ourselves by calling to mind individual members of it, it follows that when an abstract word is used, the hearer or reader has to choose, from his stock of images, one or more by which he may figure to himself the genus mentioned. In doing this, some delay must arise, some force be expended; and if by employing a specific term an appropriate image can be at once suggested, an economy is achieved, and a more vivid impression produced.”</p>
<p dir="auto">Herbert Spencer, from whose Philosophy of Style the preceding paragraph is quoted, illustrates the principle by the sentences:</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>In proportion as the manners, customs, and amusements of a nation are cruel and barbarous, the regulations of their penal code will be severe.</td>
<td>In proportion as men delight in battles, bull-fights, and combats of gladiators, will they punish by hanging, burning, and the rack.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 13. Omit needless words.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-13-omit-needless-words" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 13. Omit needless words." href="#rule-13-omit-needless-words"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Vigorous writing is concise. A sentence should contain no unnecessary words, a paragraph no unnecessary sentences, for the same reason that a drawing should have no unnecessary lines and a machine no unnecessary parts. This requires not that the writer make all his sentences short, or that he avoid all detail and treat his subjects only in outline, but that he make every word tell.</p>
<p dir="auto">Many expressions in common use violate this principle:</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>the question as to whether</td>
<td>whether (the question whether)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>there is no doubt but that</td>
<td>no doubt (doubtless)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>used for fuel purposes</td>
<td>used for fuel</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>he is a man who</td>
<td>he</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>in a hasty manner</td>
<td>hastily</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>this is a subject which</td>
<td>this subject</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>His story is a strange one.</td>
<td>His story is strange.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">In especial the expression <em>the fact that</em> should be revised out of every sentence in which it occurs.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>owing to the fact that</td>
<td>since (because)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>in spite of the fact that</td>
<td>though (although)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>call your attention to the fact that</td>
<td>remind you (notify you)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>I was unaware of the fact that</td>
<td>I was unaware that (did not know)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>the fact that he had not succeeded</td>
<td>his failure</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>the fact that I had arrived</td>
<td>my arrival</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">See also under <em>case</em>, <em>character</em>, <em>nature</em>, <em>system</em> in Chapter V.</p>
<p dir="auto"><em>Who is</em>, <em>which was</em>, and the like are often superfluous.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>His brother, who is a member of the same firm</td>
<td>His brother, a member of the same firm</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Trafalgar, which was Nelson's last battle</td>
<td>Trafalgar, Nelson's last battle</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">As positive statement is more concise than negative, and the active voice more concise than the passive, many of the examples given under Rules 11 and 12 illustrate this rule as well.</p>
<p dir="auto">A common violation of conciseness is the presentation of a single complex idea, step by step, in a series of sentences or independent clauses which might to advantage be combined into one.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Macbeth was very ambitious. This led him to wish to become king of Scotland. The witches told him that this wish of his would come true. The king of Scotland at this time was Duncan. Encouraged by his wife, Macbeth murdered Duncan. He was thus enabled to succeed Duncan as king. (51 words.)</td>
<td>Encouraged by his wife, Macbeth achieved his ambition and realized the prediction of the witches by murdering Duncan and becoming king of Scotland in his place. (26 words.)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>There were several less important courses, but these were the most important, and although they did not come every day, they came often enough to keep you in such a state of mind that you never knew what your next move would be. (43 words.)</td>
<td>These, the most important courses of all, came, if not daily, at least often enough to keep one under constant strain. (21 words.)</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 14. Avoid a succession of loose sentences</h3><a id="user-content-rule-14-avoid-a-succession-of-loose-sentences" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 14. Avoid a succession of loose sentences" href="#rule-14-avoid-a-succession-of-loose-sentences"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">This rule refers especially to loose sentences of a particular type, those consisting of two co-ordinate clauses, the second introduced by a conjunction or relative. Although single sentences of this type may be unexceptionable (see under Rule 4), a series soon becomes monotonous and tedious.</p>
<p dir="auto">An unskilful writer will sometimes construct a whole paragraph of sentences of this kind, using as connectives <em>and</em>, <em>but</em>, <em>so</em>, and less frequently, <em>who</em>, <em>which</em>, <em>when</em>, <em>where</em>, and <em>while</em>, these last in non-restrictive senses (see under Rule 3).</p>
<p dir="auto">The third concert of the subscription series was given last evening, and a large audience was in attendance. Mr. Edward Appleton was the soloist, and the Boston Symphony Orchestra furnished the instrumental music. The former showed himself to be an artist of the first rank, while the latter proved itself fully deserving of its high reputation. The interest aroused by the series has been very gratifying to the Committee, and it is planned to give a similar series annually hereafter. The fourth concert will be given on Tuesday, May 10, when an equally attractive programme will be presented.</p>
<p dir="auto">Apart from its triteness and emptiness, the paragraph above is weak because of the structure of its sentences, with their mechanical symmetry and sing-song. Contrast with them the sentences in the paragraphs quoted under Rule 9, or in any piece of good English prose, as the preface (Before the Curtain) to Vanity Fair.</p>
<p dir="auto">If the writer finds that he has written a series of sentences of the type described, he should recast enough of them to remove the monotony, replacing them by simple sentences, by sentences of two clauses joined by a semicolon, by periodic sentences of two clauses, by sentences, loose or periodic, of three clauses—whichever best represent the real relations of the thought.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 15. Express co-ordinate ideas in similar form.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-15-express-co-ordinate-ideas-in-similar-form" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 15. Express co-ordinate ideas in similar form." href="#rule-15-express-co-ordinate-ideas-in-similar-form"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">This principle, that of parallel construction, requires that expressions of similar content and function should be outwardly similar. The likeness of form enables the reader to recognize more readily the likeness of content and function. Familiar instances from the Bible are the Ten Commandments, the Beatitudes, and the petitions of the Lord's Prayer.</p>
<p dir="auto">The unskillful writer often violates this principle, from a mistaken belief that he should constantly vary the form of his expressions. It is true that in repeating a statement in order to emphasize it he may have need to vary its form. For illustration, see the paragraph from Stevenson quoted under Rule <em>9</em>. But apart from this, he should follow the principle of parallel construction.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Formerly, science was taught by the textbook method, while now the laboratory method is employed.</td>
<td>Formerly, science was taught by the textbook method; now it is taught by the laboratory method.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">The left-hand version gives the impression that the writer is undecided or timid; he seems unable or afraid to choose one form of expression and hold to it. The right-hand version shows that the writer has at least made his choice and abided by it.</p>
<p dir="auto">By this principle, an article or a preposition applying to all the members of a series must either be used only before the first term or else be repeated before each term.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>The French, the Italians, Spanish, and Portuguese</td>
<td>The French, the Italians, the Spanish, and the Portuguese</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>In spring, summer, or in winter</td>
<td>In spring, summer, or winter (In spring, in summer, or in winter)</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">Correlative expressions (<em>both, and</em>; <em>not, but</em>; <em>not only, but also</em>; <em>either, or</em>; <em>first, second, third</em>; and the like) should be followed by the same grammatical construction, that is, virtually, by the same part of speech. (Such combinations as “both Henry and I,” “not silk, but a cheap substitute,” are obviously within the rule.) Many violations of this rule (as the first three below) arise from faulty arrangement; others (as the last) from the use of unlike constructions.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>It was both a long ceremony and very tedious.</td>
<td>The ceremony was both long and tedious.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>A time not for words, but action.</td>
<td>A time not for words, but for action.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Either you must grant his request or incur his ill will.</td>
<td>You must either grant his request or incur his ill will.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>My objections are, first, the injustice of the measure; second, that it is unconstitutional.</td>
<td>My objections are, first, that the measure is unjust; second, that it is unconstitutional.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">See also the third example under Rule 12 and the last under Rule 13.</p>
<p dir="auto">It may be asked, what if a writer needs to express a very large number of similar ideas, say twenty? Must he write twenty consecutive sentences of the same pattern? On closer examination he will probably find that the difficulty is imaginary, that his twenty ideas can be classified in groups, and that he need apply the principle only within each group. Otherwise he had best avoid difficulty by putting his statements in the form of a table.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 16. Keep related words together.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-16-keep-related-words-together" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 16. Keep related words together." href="#rule-16-keep-related-words-together"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">The position of the words in a sentence is the principal means of showing their relationship. The writer must therefore, so far as possible, bring together the words, and groups of words, that are related in thought, and keep apart those which are not so related.</p>
<p dir="auto">The subject of a sentence and the principal verb should not, as a rule, be separated by a phrase or clause that can be transferred to the beginning.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Wordsworth, in the fifth book of The Excursion, gives a minute description of this church.</td>
<td>In the fifth book of The Excursion, Wordsworth gives a minute description of this church.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Cast iron, when treated in a Bessemer converter, is changed into steel.</td>
<td>By treatment in a Bessemer converter, cast iron is changed into steel.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">The objection is that the interposed phrase or clause needlessly interrupts the natural order of the main clause. Usually, however, this objection does not hold when the order is interrupted only by a relative clause or by an expression in apposition. Nor does it hold in periodic sentences in which the interruption is a deliberately used means of creating suspense (see examples under Rule 18).</p>
<p dir="auto">The relative pronoun should come, as a rule, immediately after its antecedent.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>There was a look in his eye that boded mischief.</td>
<td>In his eye was a look that boded mischief.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He wrote three articles about his adventures in Spain, which were published in Harper's Magazine.</td>
<td>He published in Harper's Magazine three articles about his adventures in Spain.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>This is a portrait of Benjamin Harrison, grandson of William Henry Harrison, who became President in 1889.</td>
<td>This is a portrait of Benjamin Harrison, grandson of William Henry Harrison. He became President in 1889.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">If the antecedent consists of a group of words, the relative comes at the end of the group, unless this would cause ambiguity.</p>
<p dir="auto">The Superintendent of the Chicago Division, who</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>A proposal to amend the Sherman Act, which has been variously judged.</td>
<td>A proposal, which has been variously judged, to amend the Sherman Act.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>—</td>
<td>A proposal to amend the much-debated Sherman Act.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The grandson of William Henry Harrison, who</td>
<td>William Henry Harrison's grandson, who</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">A noun in apposition may come between antecedent and relative, because in such a combination no real ambiguity can arise.</p>
<p dir="auto">The Duke of York, his brother, who was regarded with hostility by the Whigs</p>
<p dir="auto">Modifiers should come, if possible, next to the word they modify. If several expressions modify the same word, they should be so arranged that no wrong relation is suggested.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>All the members were not present.</td>
<td>Not all the members were present.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He only found two mistakes.</td>
<td>He found only two mistakes.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Major R. E. Joyce will give a lecture on Tuesday evening in Bailey Hall, to which the public is invited, on “My Experiences in Mesopotamia” at eight P. M.</td>
<td>On Tuesday evening at eight P. M., Major R. E. Joyce will give in Bailey Hall a lecture on “My Experiences in Mesopotamia.” The public is invited.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 17. In summaries, keep to one tense.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-17-in-summaries-keep-to-one-tense" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 17. In summaries, keep to one tense." href="#rule-17-in-summaries-keep-to-one-tense"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">In summarizing the action of a drama, the writer should always use the present tense. In summarizing a poem, story, or novel, he should preferably use the present, though he may use the past if he prefers. If the summary is in the present tense, antecedent action should be expressed by the perfect; if in the past, by the past perfect.</p>
<p dir="auto">An unforeseen chance prevents Friar John from delivering Friar Lawrence's letter to Romeo. Meanwhile, owing to her father's arbitrary change of the day set for her wedding, Juliet has been compelled to drink the potion on Tuesday night, with the result that Balthasar informs Romeo of her supposed death before Friar Lawrence learns of the non-delivery of the letter.</p>
<p dir="auto">But whichever tense be used in the summary, a past tense in indirect discourse or in indirect question remains unchanged.</p>
<p dir="auto">The Friar confesses that it was he who married them.</p>
<p dir="auto">Apart from the exceptions noted, whichever tense the writer chooses, he should use throughout. Shifting from one tense to the other gives the appearance of uncertainty and irresolution (compare Rule 15).</p>
<p dir="auto">In presenting the statements or the thought of some one else, as in summarizing an essay or reporting a speech, the writer should avoid intercalating such expressions as “he said,” “he stated,” “the speaker added,” “the speaker then went on to say,” “the author also thinks,” or the like. He should indicate clearly at the outset, once for all, that what follows is summary, and then waste no words in repeating the notification.</p>
<p dir="auto">In notebooks, in newspapers, in handbooks of literature, summaries of one kind or another may be indispensable, and for children in primary schools it is a useful exercise to retell a story in their own words. But in the criticism or interpretation of literature the writer should be careful to avoid dropping into summary. He may find it necessary to devote one or two sentences to indicating the subject, or the opening situation, of the work he is discussing; he may cite numerous details to illustrate its qualities. But he should aim to write an orderly discussion supported by evidence, not a summary with occasional comment. Similarly, if the scope of his discussion includes a number of works, he will as a rule do better not to take them up singly in chronological order, but to aim from the beginning at establishing general conclusions.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Rule 18. Place the emphatic words of a sentence at the end.</h3><a id="user-content-rule-18-place-the-emphatic-words-of-a-sentence-at-the-end" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Rule 18. Place the emphatic words of a sentence at the end." href="#rule-18-place-the-emphatic-words-of-a-sentence-at-the-end"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">The proper place in the sentence for the word, or group of words, which the writer desires to make most prominent is usually the end.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Humanity has hardly advanced in fortitude since that time, though it has advanced in many other ways.</td>
<td>Humanity, since that time, has advanced in many other ways, but it has hardly advanced in fortitude.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>This steel is principally used for making razors, because of its hardness.</td>
<td>Because of its hardness, this steel is principally used in making razors.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">The word or group of words entitled to this position of prominence is usually the logical predicate, that is, the <em>new</em> element in the sentence, as it is in the second example.</p>
<p dir="auto">The effectiveness of the periodic sentence arises from the prominence which it gives to the main statement.</p>
<p dir="auto">Four centuries ago, Christopher Columbus, one of the Italian mariners whom the decline of their own republics had put at the service of the world and of adventure, seeking for Spain a westward passage to the Indies as a set-off against the achievements of Portuguese discoverers, lighted on America.</p>
<p dir="auto">With these hopes and in this belief I would urge you, laying aside all hindrance, thrusting away all private aims, to devote yourself unswervingly and unflinchingly to the vigorous and successful prosecution of this war.</p>
<p dir="auto">The other prominent position in the sentence is the beginning. Any element in the sentence, other than the subject, may become emphatic when placed first.</p>
<p dir="auto">Deceit or treachery he could never forgive.</p>
<p dir="auto">So vast and rude, fretted by the action of nearly three thousand years, the fragments of this architecture may often seem, at first sight, like works of nature.</p>
<p dir="auto">A subject coming first in its sentence may be emphatic, but hardly by its position alone. In the sentence,</p>
<p dir="auto">Great kings worshipped at his shrine,</p>
<p dir="auto">the emphasis upon <em>kings</em> arises largely from its meaning and from the context. To receive special emphasis, the subject of a sentence must take the position of the predicate.</p>
<p dir="auto">Through the middle of the valley flowed a winding stream.</p>
<p dir="auto">The principle that the proper place for what is to be made most prominent is the end applies equally to the words of a sentence, to the sentences of a paragraph, and to the paragraphs of a composition.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">V. Words And Expressions Commonly Misused</h2><a id="user-content-v-words-and-expressions-commonly-misused" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: V. Words And Expressions Commonly Misused" href="#v-words-and-expressions-commonly-misused"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">(Some of the forms here listed, as <em>like I did</em>, are downright bad English; others, as the split infinitive, have their defenders, but are in such general disfavor that it is at least inadvisable to use them; still others, as <em>case</em>, <em>factor</em>, <em>feature</em>, <em>interesting</em>, <em>one of the most</em>, are good in their place, but are constantly obtruding themselves into places where they have no right to be. If the writer will make it his purpose from the beginning to express accurately his own individual thought, and will refuse to be satisfied with a ready-made formula that saves him the trouble of doing so, this last set of expressions will cause him little trouble. But if he finds that in a moment of inadvertence he has used one of them, his proper course will probably be not to patch up the sentence by substituting one word or set of words for another, but to recast it completely, as illustrated in a number of examples below and in others under Rules 12 and 13.)</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>All right.</strong> Idiomatic in familiar speech as a detached phrase in the sense, “Agreed,” or “Go ahead.” In other uses better avoided. Always written as two words.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>As good or better than.</strong> Expressions of this type should be corrected by rearranging the sentence.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>My opinion is as good or better than his.</td>
<td>My opinion is as good as his, or better (if not better).</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>As to whether.</strong> <em>Whether</em> is sufficient; see under Rule 13.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Bid.</strong> Takes the infinitive without <em>to</em>. The past tense in the sense, <em>“ordered,”</em> is <em>bade</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>But.</strong> Unnecessary after <em>doubt</em> and <em>help</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>I have no doubt but that</td>
<td>I have no doubt that</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He could not help see but that</td>
<td>He could not help seeing that</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">The too frequent use of <em>but</em> as a conjunction leads to the fault discussed under Rule 14. A loose sentence formed with <em>but</em> can always be converted into a periodic sentence formed with <em>although</em>, as illustrated under Rule 4.</p>
<p dir="auto">Particularly awkward is the following of one <em>but</em> by another, making a contrast to a contrast or a reservation to a reservation. This is easily corrected by re-arrangement.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>America had vast resources, but she seemed almost wholly unprepared for war. But within a year she had created an army of four million men.</td>
<td>America seemed almost wholly unprepared for war, but she had vast resources. Within a year she had created an army of four million men.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Can.</strong> Means <em>am (is, are) able</em>. Not to be used as a substitute for <em>may</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Case.</strong> The Concise Oxford Dictionary begins its definition of this word: “instance of a thing's occurring; usual state of affairs.” In these two senses, the word is usually unnecessary.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>In many cases, the rooms were poorly ventilated.</td>
<td>Many of the rooms were poorly ventilated.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>It has rarely been the case that any mistake has been made.</td>
<td>Few mistakes have been made.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">See Wood, Suggestions to Authors, pp. 68–71, and Quiller-Couch, The Art of Writing, pp. 103–106.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Certainly.</strong> Used indiscriminately by some writers, much as others use <em>very</em>, to intensify any and every statement. A mannerism of this kind, bad in speech, is even worse in writing.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Character.</strong> Often simply redundant, used from a mere habit of wordiness.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Acts of a hostile character</td>
<td>Hostile acts</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Claim, vb.</strong> With object-noun, means <em>lay claim to</em>. May be used with a dependent clause if this sense is clearly involved: “He claimed that he was the sole surviving heir.” (But even here, “claimed to be” would be better.) Not to be used as a substitute for <em>declare</em>, <em>maintain</em>, or <em>charge</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Clever.</strong> This word has been greatly overused; it is best restricted to ingenuity displayed in small matters.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Compare.</strong> To <em>compare to</em> is to point out or imply resemblances, between objects regarded as essentially of different order; to <em>compare with</em> is mainly to point out differences, between objects regarded as essentially of the same order. Thus life has been compared to a pilgrimage, to a drama, to a battle; Congress may be compared with the British Parliament. Paris has been compared to ancient Athens; it may be compared with modern London.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Consider.</strong> Not followed by <em>as</em> when it means “believe to be.” “I consider him thoroughly competent.” Compare, “The lecturer considered Cromwell first as soldier and second as administrator,” where “considered” means “examined” or “discussed.”</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Data.</strong> A plural, like <em>phenomena</em> and <em>strata</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto">These data were tabulated.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Dependable.</strong> A needless substitute for <em>reliable</em>, <em>trustworthy</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Different than.</strong> Not permissible. Substitute <em>different from</em>, <em>other than</em>, or <em>unlike</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Divided into.</strong> Not to be misused for <em>composed of</em>. The line is sometimes difficult to draw; doubtless plays are divided into acts, but poems are composed of stanzas.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Don't.</strong> Contraction of <em>do not</em>. The contraction of <em>does not</em> is <em>doesn't</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Due to.</strong> Incorrectly used for <em>through</em>, <em>because of</em>, or <em>owing to</em>, in adverbial phrases: “He lost the first game, due to carelessness.” In correct use related as predicate or as modifier to a particular noun: “This invention is due to Edison;” “losses due to preventable fires.”</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Folk.</strong> A collective noun, equivalent to <em>people</em>. Use the singular form only.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Effect.</strong> As noun, means <em>result</em>; as verb, means <em><em>to</em> bring about</em>, <em>accomplish</em> (not to be confused with <em>affect</em>, which means “to influence”).</p>
<p dir="auto">As noun, often loosely used in perfunctory writing about fashions, music, painting, and other arts: “an Oriental effect;” “effects in pale green;” “very delicate effects;” “broad effects;” “subtle effects;” “a charming effect was produced by.” The writer who has a definite meaning to express will not take refuge in such vagueness.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Etc.</strong> Equivalent to <em>and the rest</em>, <em>and so forth</em>, and hence not to be used if one of these would be insufficient, that is, if the reader would be left in doubt as to any important particulars. Least open to objection when it represents the last terms of a list already given in full, or immaterial words at the end of a quotation.</p>
<p dir="auto">At the end of a list introduced by <em>such as</em>, <em>for example</em>, or any similar expression, <em>etc.</em> is incorrect.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Fact.</strong> Use this word only of matters of a kind capable of direct verification, not of matters of judgment. That a particular event happened on a given date, that lead melts at a certain temperature, are facts. But such conclusions as that Napoleon was the greatest of modern generals, or that the climate of California is delightful, however incontestable they <em>may be</em>, are not properly facts.</p>
<p dir="auto">On the formula <em>the fact that</em>, see under Rule 13.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Factor.</strong> A hackneyed word; the expressions of which it forms part can usually be replaced by something more direct and idiomatic.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>His superior training was the great factor in his winning the match.</td>
<td>He won the match by being better trained.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Heavy artillery has become an increasingly important factor in deciding battles.</td>
<td>Heavy artillery has played a constantly larger part in deciding battles.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Feature.</strong> Another hackneyed word; like <em>factor</em> it usually adds nothing to the sentence in which it occurs.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>A feature of the entertainment especially worthy of mention was the singing of Miss A.</td>
<td>(Better use the same number of words to tell what Miss A. sang, or if the programme has already been given, to tell how she sang.)</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">As a verb, in the advertising sense of <em>offer as a special attraction</em>, to be avoided.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Fix.</strong> Colloquial in America for <em>arrange</em>, <em>prepare</em>, <em>mend</em>. In writing restrict it to its literary senses, <em>fasten</em>, <em>make firm or immovable</em>, etc.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Get.</strong> The colloquial <em>have got</em> for <em>have</em> should not be used in writing. The preferable form of the participle is <em>got</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>He is a man who.</strong> A common type of redundant expression; see Rule 13.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>He is a man who is very ambitious.</td>
<td>He is very ambitious.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Spain is a country which I have always wanted to visit.</td>
<td>I have always wanted to visit Spain.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Help.</strong> See under <strong>But</strong>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>However.</strong> In the meaning <em>nevertheless</em>, not to come first in its sentence or clause.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>The roads were almost impassable. However, we at last succeeded in reaching camp.</td>
<td>The roads were almost impassable. At last, however, we succeeded in reaching camp.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">When <em>however</em> comes first, it means <em>in whatever way</em> or <em>to whatever extent</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto">However you advise him, he will probably do as he thinks best.</p>
<p dir="auto">However discouraging the prospect, he never lost heart.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Interesting.</strong> Avoid this word as a perfunctory means of introduction. Instead of announcing that what you are about to tell is interesting, make it so.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>An interesting story is told of</td>
<td>(Tell the story without preamble.)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>In connection with the anticipated visit of Mr. B. to America, it is interesting to recall that he</td>
<td>Mr. B., who it is expected will soon visit America</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Kind of.</strong> Not to be used as a substitute for <em>rather</em> (before adjectives and verbs), or except in familiar style, for <em>something like</em> (before nouns). Restrict it to its literal sense: “Amber is a kind of fossil resin;” “I dislike that kind of notoriety.” The same holds true of <em>sort of</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Less.</strong> Should not be misused for <em>fewer</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>He had less men than in the previous campaign</td>
<td>He had fewer men than in the previous campaign</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><em>Less</em> refers to quantity, <em>fewer</em> to number. “His troubles are less than mine” means “His troubles are not so great as mine.” “His troubles are fewer than mine” means “His troubles are not so numerous as mine.” It is, however, correct to say, “The signers of the petition were less than a hundred,” where the round number <em>a hundred</em> is something like a collective noun, and <em>less</em> is thought of as meaning a less quantity or amount.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Like.</strong> Not to be misused for <em>as</em>. <em>Like</em> governs nouns and pronouns; before phrases and clauses the equivalent word is <em>as</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>We spent the evening like in the old days.</td>
<td>We spent the evening as in the old days.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He thought like I did.</td>
<td>He thought as I did (like me).</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Line, along these lines.</strong> <em>Line</em> in the sense of <em>course of procedure</em>, <em>conduct</em>, <em>thought</em>, is allowable, but has been so much overworked, particularly in the phrase <em>along these lines</em>, that a writer who aims at freshness or originality had better discard it entirely.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Mr. B. also spoke along the same lines.</td>
<td>Mr. B. also spoke, to the same effect.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He is studying along the line of French literature.</td>
<td>He is studying French literature.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Literal, literally.</strong> Often incorrectly used in support of exaggeration or violent metaphor.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>A literal flood of abuse.</td>
<td>A flood of abuse.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Literally dead with fatigue</td>
<td>Almost dead with fatigue (dead tired)</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Lose out.</strong> Meant to be more emphatic than <em>lose</em>, but actually less so, because of its commonness. The same holds true of <em>try out</em>, <em>win out</em>, <em>sign up</em>, <em>register up</em>. With a number of verbs, <em>out</em> and <em>up</em> form idiomatic combinations: <em>find out</em>, <em>run out</em>, <em>turn out</em>, <em>cheer up</em>, <em>dry up</em>, <em>make up</em>, and others, each distinguishable in meaning from the simple verb. <em>Lose out</em> is not.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Most.</strong> Not to be used for <em>almost</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Most everybody</td>
<td>Almost everybody</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Most all the time</td>
<td>Almost all the time</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Nature.</strong> Often simply redundant, used like <em>character</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Acts of a hostile <em>nature</em></td>
<td>Hostile acts</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">Often vaguely used in such expressions as a “lover of nature;” “poems about nature.” Unless more specific statements follow, the reader cannot tell whether the poems have to do with natural scenery, rural life, the sunset, the untracked wilderness, or the habits of squirrels.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Near by.</strong> Adverbial phrase, not yet fully accepted as good English, though the analogy of <em>close by</em> and <em>hard by</em> seems to justify it. <em>Near</em>, or <em>near at hand</em>, is as good, if not better.</p>
<p dir="auto">Not to be used as an adjective; use <em>neighboring</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Oftentimes, ofttimes.</strong> Archaic forms, no longer in good use. The modern word is <em>often</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>One hundred and one.</strong> Retain the <em>and</em> in this and similar expressions, in accordance with the unvarying usage of English prose from Old English times.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>One of the most.</strong> Avoid beginning essays or paragraphs with this formula, as, “One of the most interesting developments of modern science is, etc.;” “Switzerland is one of the most interesting countries of Europe.” There is nothing wrong in this; it is simply threadbare and forcible-feeble.</p>
<p dir="auto">A common blunder is to use a singular verb in a relative clause following this or a similar expression, when the relative is the subject.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>One of the ablest men that has attacked this problem.</td>
<td>One of the ablest men that have attacked this problem.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Participle for verbal noun.</strong></p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Do you mind me asking a question?</td>
<td>Do you mind my asking a question?</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>There was little prospect of the Senate accepting even this compromise.</td>
<td>There was little prospect of the Senate's accepting even this compromise.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">In the left-hand column, <em>asking</em> and <em>accepting</em> are present participles; in the right-hand column, they are verbal nouns (gerunds). The construction shown in the left-hand column is occasionally found, and has its defenders. Yet it is easy to see that the second sentence has to do not with a prospect of the Senate, but with a prospect of accepting. In this example, at least, the construction is plainly illogical.</p>
<p dir="auto">As the authors of The King's English point out, there are sentences apparently, but not really, of this type, in which the possessive is not called for.</p>
<p dir="auto">I cannot imagine Lincoln refusing his assent to this measure.</p>
<p dir="auto">In this sentence, what the writer cannot imagine is Lincoln himself, in the act of refusing his assent. Yet the meaning would be virtually the same, except for a slight loss of vividness, if he had written,</p>
<p dir="auto">I cannot imagine Lincoln's refusing his assent to this measure.</p>
<p dir="auto">By using the possessive, the writer will always be on the safe side.</p>
<p dir="auto">In the examples above, the subject of the action is a single, unmodified term, immediately preceding the verbal noun, and the construction is as good as any that could be used. But in any sentence in which it is a mere clumsy substitute for something simpler, or in which the use of the possessive is awkward or impossible, should of course be recast.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>In the event of a reconsideration of the whole matter's becoming necessary</td>
<td>If it should become necessary to reconsider the whole matter</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>There was great dissatisfaction with the decision of the arbitrators being favorable to the company.</td>
<td>There was great dissatisfaction that the arbitrators should have decided in favor of the company.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>People.</strong> <em>The people</em> is a political term, not to be confused with <em>the public</em>. From the people comes political support or opposition; from the public comes artistic appreciation or commercial patronage.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Phase.</strong> Means a stage of transition or development: “the phases of the moon;” “the last phase.” Not to be used for <em>aspect</em> or <em>topic</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Another phase of the subject</td>
<td>Another point (another question)</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Possess.</strong> Not to be used as a mere substitute for <em>have</em> or <em>own</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>He possessed great courage.</td>
<td>He had great courage (was very brave).</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>He was the fortunate possessor of</td>
<td>He owned</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Prove.</strong> The past participle is <em>proved</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Respective, respectively.</strong> These words may usually be omitted with advantage.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Works of fiction are listed under the names of their respective authors.</td>
<td>Works of fiction are listed under the names of their authors.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The one mile and two mile runs were won by Jones and Cummings respectively.</td>
<td>The one mile and two mile runs were won by Jones and by Cummings.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">In some kinds of formal writing, as geometrical proofs, it may be necessary to use <em>respectively</em>, but it should not appear in writing on ordinary subjects.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Shall, Will.</strong> The future tense requires <em>shall</em> for the first person, <em>will</em> for the second and third. The formula to express the speaker's belief regarding his future action or state is <em>I shall</em>; <em>I will</em> expresses his determination or his consent.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Should.</strong> See under <strong>Would</strong>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>So.</strong> Avoid, in writing, the use of <em>so</em> as an intensifier: “so good;” “so warm;” “so delightful.”</p>
<p dir="auto">On the use of <em>so</em> to introduce clauses, see Rule 4.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Sort of.</strong> See under <strong>Kind of</strong>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Split Infinitive.</strong> There is precedent from the fourteenth century downward for interposing an adverb between <em>to</em> and the infinitive which it governs, but the construction is in disfavor and is avoided by nearly all careful writers.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>To diligently inquire</td>
<td>To inquire diligently</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>State.</strong> Not to be used as a mere substitute for <em>say</em>, <em>remark</em>. Restrict it to the sense of <em>express fully or clearly</em>, as, “He refused to state his objections.”</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Student Body.</strong> A needless and awkward expression meaning no more than the simple word <em>students</em>.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>A member of the student body</td>
<td>A student</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Popular with the student body</td>
<td>Liked by the students</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The student body passed resolutions.</td>
<td>The students passed resolutions.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>System.</strong> Frequently used without need.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Dayton has adopted the commission system of <em>government.</em></td>
<td>Dayton has adopted government by commission.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The dormitory system</td>
<td>Dormitories</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Thanking You in Advance.</strong> This sounds as if the writer meant, “It will not be worth my while to write to you again.” In making your request, write, “Will you please,” or “I shall be obliged,” and if anything further seems necessary write a letter of acknowledgment later.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>They.</strong> A common inaccuracy is the use of the plural pronoun when the antecedent is a distributive expression such as <em>each</em>, <em>each one</em>, <em>everybody</em>, <em>every one</em>, <em>many a man</em>, which, though implying more than one person, requires the pronoun to be in the singular. Similar to this, but with even less justification, is the use of the plural pronoun with the antecedent <em>anybody</em>, <em>any one</em>, <em>somebody</em>, <em>some one</em>, the intention being either to avoid the awkward “he or she,” or to avoid committing oneself to either. Some bashful speakers even say, “A friend of mine told me that they, etc.”</p>
<p dir="auto">Use <em>he</em> with all the above words, unless the antecedent is or must be feminine.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Very.</strong> Use this word sparingly. Where emphasis is necessary, use words strong in themselves.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Viewpoint.</strong> Write <em>point of view</em>, but do not misuse this, as many do, for <em>view</em> or <em>opinion</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>While.</strong> Avoid the indiscriminate use of this word for <em>and</em>, <em>but</em>, and <em>although</em>. Many writers use it frequently as a substitute for <em>and</em> or <em>but</em>, either from a mere desire to vary the connective, or from uncertainty which of the two connectives is the more appropriate. In this use it is best replaced by a semicolon.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>The office and salesrooms are on the ground floor, while the rest of the building is devoted to manufacturing.</td>
<td>The office and salesrooms are on the ground floor; the rest of the building is devoted to manufacturing.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">Its use as a virtual equivalent of <em>although</em> is allowable in sentences where this leads to no ambiguity or absurdity.</p>
<p dir="auto">While I admire his energy, I wish it were employed in a better cause.</p>
<p dir="auto">This is entirely correct, as shown by the paraphrase,</p>
<p dir="auto">I admire his energy; at the same time I wish it were employed in a better cause.</p>
<p dir="auto">Compare:</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>While the temperature reaches 90 or 95 degrees in the daytime, the nights are often chilly.</td>
<td>Although the temperature reaches 90 or 95 degrees in the daytime, the nights are often chilly.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">The paraphrase,</p>
<p dir="auto">The temperature reaches 90 or 95 degrees in the daytime; at the same time the nights are often chilly,</p>
<p dir="auto">shows why the use of <em>while</em> is incorrect.</p>
<p dir="auto">In general, the writer will do well to use <em>while</em> only with strict literalness, in the sense of <em>during the time that</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Whom.</strong> Often incorrectly used for <em>who</em> before <em>he said</em> or similar expressions, when it is really the subject of a following verb.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>His brother, whom he said would send him the money</td>
<td>His brother, who he said would send him the money</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The man whom he thought was his friend</td>
<td>The man who (that) he thought was his friend (whom he thought his friend)</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Worth while.</strong> Overworked as a term of vague approval and (with <em>not</em>) of disapproval. Strictly applicable only to actions: “Is it worth while to telegraph?”</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>His books are not worth while.</td>
<td>His books are not worth reading (are not worth one's while to read; do not repay reading; are worthless).</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">The use of <em>worth while</em> before a noun (“a worth while story”) is indefensible.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Would.</strong> A conditional statement in the first person requires <em>should</em>, not <em>would</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto">I should not have succeeded without his help.</p>
<p dir="auto">The equivalent of <em>shall</em> in indirect quotation after a verb in the past tense is <em>should</em>, not <em>would</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto">He predicted that before long we should have a great surprise.</p>
<p dir="auto">To express habitual or repeated action, the past tense, without <em>would</em>, is usually sufficient, and from its brevity, more emphatic.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Original</th>
<th>Revision</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Once a year he would visit the old mansion.</td>
<td>Once a year he visited the old mansion.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
